<p><i><b>Sesame Street</b></i> is a long-running American <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_television_series" title="Children's television series">children's television series</a>, produced by <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Workshop" title="Sesame Workshop">Sesame Workshop</a> (formerly known as the Children's Television Workshop) and created by <a href="/wiki/Joan_Ganz_Cooney" title="Joan Ganz Cooney">Joan Ganz Cooney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Morrisett" title="Lloyd Morrisett">Lloyd Morrisett</a>. The program is known for its educational content, and images communicated through the use of <a href="/wiki/Jim_Henson" title="Jim Henson">Jim Henson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Muppets" title="The Muppets">Muppets</a>, animation, short films, humor, and cultural references. The series premiered on November 10, 1969, to positive reviews, some controversy, and high viewership; it has aired on the U.S.'s national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_television" title="Public television">public television</a> provider (<a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a>) since its debut, with its first run moving to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premium_channel" title="Premium channel">premium channel</a> <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> on January 16, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-season46_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-season46-2">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The show has undergone significant changes throughout its history. The format of <i>Sesame Street</i> consists of a combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_television" title="Commercial television">commercial television</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_production" title="Television production">production</a> elements and techniques which have evolved to reflect the changes in American culture and the audience's viewing habits. With the creation of <i>Sesame Street</i>, producers and writers of a children's television show used, for the first time, educational goals and a <a href="/wiki/Curriculum" title="Curriculum">curriculum</a> to shape its content. It was also the first time a show's educational effects were studied.</p>
<p>Shortly after creating <i>Sesame Street</i>, its producers developed what came to be called the "CTW model" (after the production company's previous name), a system of television show planning, production, and evaluation based on collaborations between producers, writers, educators, and researchers. The show was initially funded by government and private foundations but has become somewhat self-supporting due to revenues from licensing arrangements, international sales, and other media. By 2006, there were independently produced versions, or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_co-productions_of_Sesame_Street" title="International co-productions of Sesame Street">co-productions</a>", of <i>Sesame Street</i> broadcast in twenty countries. In 2001 there were over 120 million viewers of various international versions of <i>Sesame Street</i>, and by the show's 40th anniversary in 2009, it was broadcast in more than 140 countries.</p>
<p>By its 40th anniversary in 2009, <i>Sesame Street</i> was the fifteenth-highest-rated children's television show in the United States. A 1996 survey found that 95% of all American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preschoolers" title="Preschoolers">preschoolers</a> had watched the show by the time they were three years old. In 2008, it was estimated that 77 million Americans had watched the series as children. As of 2014, <i>Sesame Street</i> has won 167 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmy_Awards" title="Emmy Awards">Emmy Awards</a> and 8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammy_Awards" title="Grammy Awards">Grammy Awards</a>—more than any other children's show.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sesame_Street" title="History of Sesame Street">History of Sesame Street</a></div>
<p><i>Sesame Street</i> was conceived in 1966 during discussions between television producer Joan Ganz Cooney and <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Corporation_of_New_York" title="Carnegie Corporation of New York">Carnegie Foundation</a> vice president Lloyd Morrisett. Their goal was to create a children's television show that would "master the addictive qualities of television and do something good with them",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-8_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-8-3">[2]</a></sup> such as helping young children prepare for school. After two years of research the newly formed Children's Television Workshop (CTW) received a combined grant of US$8 million ($52 million in 2017 dollars)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-4">[3]</a></sup> from the Carnegie Foundation, the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Foundation" title="Ford Foundation">Ford Foundation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. Federal Government</a> to create and produce a new children's television show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finch.2C_p._53_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finch.2C_p._53-5">[4]</a></sup> The program premiered on <a href="/wiki/Public_broadcasting" title="Public broadcasting">public television</a> stations on November 10, 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brooke_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-brooke-6">[5]</a></sup> It was the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preschool_education" title="Preschool education">preschool educational</a> television program to base its contents and production values on laboratory and formative research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-7">[6]</a></sup> Initial responses to the show included adulatory reviews, some controversy, and high ratings. By its 40th anniversary in 2009, <i>Sesame Street</i> was broadcast in over 120 countries, and 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_co-productions_of_Sesame_Street" title="International co-productions of Sesame Street">international versions</a> had been produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friedman_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-friedman-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"I've always said of our original team that developed and produced <i>Sesame Street</i>: Collectively, we were a genius."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—<i>Sesame Street</i> creator <a href="/wiki/Joan_Ganz_Cooney" title="Joan Ganz Cooney">Joan Ganz Cooney</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-26_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-26-9">[8]</a></sup></cite></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG"><img alt="Black and white photo of a smiling woman about fifty years of age and wearing a jacket and tied-up scarf" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="455" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG/220px-Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG/330px-Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG/440px-Joan_Ganz_Cooney.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3468" data-file-width="5212" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg/220px-Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg/330px-Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg/440px-Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lloyd_Morrisett_and_his_birthday_cupcakes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lloyd Morrisett, Co-creator</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Sesame Street</i> has evolved from its initial inception. According to writer Michael Davis, by the mid-1970s the show had become "an American institution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-220_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-220-10">[9]</a></sup> The cast and crew expanded during this time, with emphasis on the hiring of women crew members and the addition of minorities to the cast. The show's success continued into the 1980s. In 1981, when the federal government withdrew its funding, CTW turned to, and expanded, other revenue sources, including its magazine division, book <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royalties" title="Royalties">royalties</a>, product licensing, and foreign broadcast income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-odell_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-odell-11">[10]</a></sup> <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s curriculum has expanded to include more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affective" title="Affective">affective</a> topics such as relationships, ethics, and emotions. Many of the show's storylines were taken from the experiences of its writing staff, cast, and crew, most notably, the 1982 death of <a href="/wiki/Will_Lee" title="Will Lee">Will Lee</a>—who played <a href="/wiki/Mr._Hooper" title="Mr. Hooper">Mr. Hooper</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hellman-52_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-hellman-52-12">[11]</a></sup>—and the marriage of Luis and Maria in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borgenicht.2C_p._80_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borgenicht.2C_p._80-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent years <i>Sesame Street</i> has faced societal and economic challenges, including changes in viewing habits of young children, competition from other shows, the development of cable television, and a drop in ratings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-320_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-320-14">[13]</a></sup> After the turn of the 21st century, <i>Sesame Street</i> made major structural changes. For example, starting in 2002, its format became more narrative and included ongoing storylines. After its thirtieth anniversary in 1999 and due to the popularity of the Muppet <a href="/wiki/Elmo" title="Elmo">Elmo</a>, the show also incorporated a popular segment known as "<a href="/wiki/Elmo%27s_World" title="Elmo's World">Elmo's World</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodman_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-goodman-15">[14]</a></sup> Upon its fortieth anniversary in 2009, the show received a Lifetime Achievement Emmy at the <a href="/wiki/36th_Daytime_Emmy_Awards" title="36th Daytime Emmy Awards">36th Daytime Emmy Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmys_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-emmys-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 13, 2015, as part of a five-year programming and development deal, Sesame Workshop announced that first-run episodes of <i>Sesame Street</i> would move to premium television service <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> beginning with season 46, which premiered on January 16, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-season46_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-season46-2">[1]</a></sup> HBO will hold first-run rights to all newer episodes of the series, after which they will air on PBS member stations following a nine-month exclusivity window, with no charge to the stations for airing the content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The agreement also gives HBO exclusive rights to stream past and future <i>Sesame Street</i> episodes on <a href="/wiki/HBO_Go" title="HBO Go">HBO Go</a> and <a href="/wiki/HBO_Now" title="HBO Now">HBO Now</a> – assuming those rights from <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Video" title="Amazon Video">Amazon Video</a> and <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a>; on August 14, Sesame Workshop announced that it would phase out its in-house subscription streaming service, Sesame Go, as a standalone service; the service will remain in operation, likely with its offerings reduced to a slate content available for free or serving as a portal for <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> The deal came in the wake of cutbacks that had affected the series in recent years, the changing viewer habits of American children in the previous ten years, and Sesame Workshop's dependence upon revenue from DVD sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Format">Format</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Format_of_Sesame_Street" title="Format of Sesame Street">Format of Sesame Street</a></div>
<p>From its first episode, <i>Sesame Street</i> has structured its format by using "a strong visual style, fast-moving action, humor, and music," as well as animation and live-action short films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-odell-70_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-odell-70-21">[20]</a></sup> When <i>Sesame Street</i> premiered, most researchers believed that young children did not have long <a href="/wiki/Attention_span" title="Attention span">attention spans</a>, therefore the new show's producers were concerned that an hour-long show would not hold their audience's attention. At first, the show's "street scenes"—the action taking place on its set—consisted of character-driven interactions and were not written as ongoing stories. Instead, they consisted of individual, curriculum-based segments which were interrupted by "inserts" consisting of puppet sketches, short films, and animations. This structure allowed the producers to use a mixture of styles and characters, and to vary the show's pace. By season 20, research had shown that children were able to follow a story, and the street scenes, while still interspersed with other segments, became evolving storylines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magazine_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-magazine-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-179_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-179-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"We basically deconstructed the show. It's not a magazine format anymore. It's more like the <i>Sesame</i> hour. Children will be able to navigate through it easier."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—Executive producer Arlene Sherman, speaking of the show's restructuring in 2002<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timgoodman_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-timgoodman-24">[23]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Upon recommendations by <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">child psychologists</a>, the producers initially decided that the show's human actors and Muppets would not interact because they were concerned it would confuse young children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fisch-39_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-fisch-39-25">[24]</a></sup> When the CTW tested the appeal of the new show, they found that although children paid attention to the shows during the Muppet segments, their interest was lost during the "Street" segments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gladwell-105_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-gladwell-105-26">[25]</a></sup> The producers requested that Henson and his team create Muppets such as <a href="/wiki/Big_Bird" title="Big Bird">Big Bird</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oscar_the_Grouch" title="Oscar the Grouch">Oscar the Grouch</a> to interact with the human actors, and the Street segments were re-shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladwell.2C_p._106_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gladwell.2C_p._106-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fisch3940_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-fisch3940-28">[27]</a></sup> <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s format remained intact until the show's later decades, when the changing audience required that producers move to a more narrative format. In 1998 the popular segment "Elmo's World", a 15-minute-long segment hosted by the Muppet Elmo, was created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clash-75_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-clash-75-29">[28]</a></sup> Starting in 2014, during the show's 45th season, the producers introduced a bonus half-hour version of the program. The new version, which complemented the full-hour series, was both broadcast weekday afternoons and streamed on the internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halfhour_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-halfhour-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_goals">Educational goals</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Educational_goals_of_Sesame_Street" title="Educational goals of Sesame Street">Educational goals of Sesame Street</a></div>
<p>As author <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Gladwell" title="Malcolm Gladwell">Malcolm Gladwell</a> has stated, "<i>Sesame Street</i> was built around a single, breakthrough insight: that if you can hold the attention of children, you can educate them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gladwell-100_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-gladwell-100-31">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gerald_S._Lesser" title="Gerald S. Lesser">Gerald S. Lesser</a>, the CTW's first advisory board chair, went even further, saying that the effective use of television as an educational tool needed to capture, focus, and sustain children's attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lesser.2C_p._116_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lesser.2C_p._116-32">[31]</a></sup> <i>Sesame Street</i> was the first children's show to structure each episode, and the segments within them, to capture children's attention, and to make, as Gladwell put it, "small but critical adjustments" to keep it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gladwell-91_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-gladwell-91-33">[32]</a></sup> According to CTW researchers Rosemarie Truglio and Shalom Fisch, <i>Sesame Street</i> was one of the few children's television programs to utilize a detailed and comprehensive educational curriculum, garnered from <a href="/wiki/Formative_assessment" title="Formative assessment">formative</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summative" title="Summative">summative</a> research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truglio-234_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-truglio-234-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The creators of <i>Sesame Street</i> and their researchers formulated both <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Affect_(psychology)" title="Affect (psychology)">affective</a> goals for the show. Initially, they focused on cognitive goals, while addressing affective goals indirectly, in the belief that doing so would increase children's self-esteem and feelings of competency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-76_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-76-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-106_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-106-36">[35]</a></sup> One of their primary goals was preparing very young children for school, especially children from <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">low-income</a> families,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-46_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-46-37">[36]</a></sup> using <a href="/wiki/Modeling_(psychology)" title="Modeling (psychology)">modeling</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser8687_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser8687-38">[37]</a></sup> repetition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-107_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-107-39">[38]</a></sup> and humor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lesser.2C_p._116_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lesser.2C_p._116-32">[31]</a></sup> to fulfill these goals. They made changes in the show's content to increase their viewers' attention and to increase its appeal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-87_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-87-40">[39]</a></sup> and encouraged "co-viewing" to entice older children and parents to watch the show by including more sophisticated humor, cultural references, and celebrity guest appearances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allshow_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-allshow-41">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>After <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s first season, its critics forced its producers and researchers to address more overtly such affective goals as social competence, tolerance of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_diversity" title="Cultural diversity">diversity</a>, and nonaggressive ways of resolving conflict. These issues were addressed through interpersonal disputes among its Street characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huston_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-huston-42">[41]</a></sup> During the 1980s, the show incorporated the real-life experiences of the show's cast and crew, including the death of <a href="/wiki/Will_Lee" title="Will Lee">Will Lee</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mr._Hooper" title="Mr. Hooper">Mr. Hooper</a>) and the pregnancy of <a href="/wiki/Sonia_Manzano" title="Sonia Manzano">Sonia Manzano</a> (Maria) to address affective concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hellman-52_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-hellman-52-12">[11]</a></sup> In later seasons, <i>Sesame Street</i> addressed real-life disasters such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11_terrorist_attacks" title="September 11 terrorist attacks">September 11 terrorist attacks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-165_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-165-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The show's goals for outreach were addressed through a series of programs that first focused on promotion and then, after the first season, on the development of educational materials used in preschool settings. Innovative programs were developed because their target audience, children and their families in low-income, inner-city homes, did not traditionally watch educational programs on television and because traditional methods of promotion and advertising were not effective with these groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-181_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-181-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span></h2>
<p>As a result of Cooney's initial proposal in 1968, the Carnegie Institute awarded her an $8 million ($52 million in 2017 dollars)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-4">[3]</a></sup> grant to create a new children's television program and establish the CTW,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finch.2C_p._53_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Finch.2C_p._53-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer-3_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-3-45">[44]</a></sup> renamed in June 5, 2000 to <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Workshop" title="Sesame Workshop">Sesame Workshop</a> (SW).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Cooney and Morrisett procured additional multimillion-dollar grants from the U.S. federal government, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Arthur_Vining_Davis_Foundations" title="The Arthur Vining Davis Foundations">The Arthur Vining Davis Foundations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporation_for_Public_Broadcasting" title="Corporation for Public Broadcasting">CPB</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Foundation" title="Ford Foundation">Ford Foundation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-8_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis-8-3">[2]</a></sup> Davis reported that Cooney and Morrisett decided that if they did not procure full funding from the beginning, they would drop the idea of producing the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-105_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-105-47">[46]</a></sup> As Lesser reported, funds gained from a combination of government agencies and private foundations protected them from the economic pressures experienced by commercial broadcast television networks, but created challenges in procuring future funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-17_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-17-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>After <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s initial success, its producers began to think about its survival beyond its development and first season and decided to explore other funding sources. From the first season, they understood that the source of their funding, which they considered "seed" money, would need to be replaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-203_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-203-49">[48]</a></sup> The 1970s were marked by conflicts between the CTW and the federal government; in 1978, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Education" title="United States Department of Education">U.S. Department of Education</a> refused to deliver a $2 million check until the last day of CTW's fiscal year. As a result, the CTW decided to depend upon licensing arrangements with toy companies and other manufacturers, publishing, and international sales for their funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-odell_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-odell-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1998, the CTW accepted <a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">corporate sponsorship</a> to raise funds for <i>Sesame Street</i> and other projects. For the first time, they allowed short advertisements by indoor playground manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Zone" title="Discovery Zone">Discovery Zone</a>, their first corporate sponsor, to air before and after each episode. Consumer advocate <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a>, who had previously appeared on <i>Sesame Street</i>, called for a boycott of the show, saying that the CTW was "exploiting impressionable children".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brooke_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-brooke-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street_research" title="Sesame Street research">Sesame Street research</a></div>
<p>Producer Joan Ganz Cooney has stated, "Without research, there would be no <i>Sesame Street</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooney-xi_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-cooney-xi-50">[49]</a></sup> In 1967, when Cooney and her team began to plan the show's development, combining research with television production was, as she put it, "positively heretical".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooney-xi_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-cooney-xi-50">[49]</a></sup> Shortly after creating <i>Sesame Street</i>, its producers began to develop what came to be called "the CTW model", a system of planning, production, and evaluation that did not fully emerge until the end of the show's first season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-68_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-68-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[note 2]</a></sup> According to Morrow, the CTW model consisted of four parts: "the interaction of receptive television producers and <a href="/wiki/Child_development" title="Child development">child science</a> experts, the creation of a specific and age-appropriate curriculum, research to shape the program directly, and independent measurement of viewers' learning".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-68_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-68-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cooney credited the show's high standard in research procedures to Harvard professors <a href="/wiki/Gerald_S._Lesser" title="Gerald S. Lesser">Gerald S. Lesser</a>, whom the CTW hired to design the show's educational objectives, and <a href="/wiki/Edward_L._Palmer" title="Edward L. Palmer">Edward L. Palmer</a>, who was responsible for conducting the show's formative research and for bridging the gap between the show's producers and researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooney-xii_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-cooney-xii-53">[51]</a></sup> The CTW conducted research in two ways: in-house formative research that informed and improved production,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mielke-8485_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-mielke-8485-54">[52]</a></sup> and independent summative evaluations, conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Educational_Testing_Service" title="Educational Testing Service">Educational Testing Service</a> (ETS) during the show's first two seasons, which measuring its educational effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-7">[6]</a></sup> Cooney stated, "From the beginning, we—the planners of the project—designed the show as an experimental research project with educational advisers, researchers, and television producers collaborating as equal partners".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borgenicht.2C_p._9_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borgenicht.2C_p._9-55">[53]</a></sup> Cooney also described the collaboration as an "arranged marriage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooney-xi_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-cooney-xi-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span></h3>
<p><i>Sesame Street</i> has used many writers in its long history. As Dave Connell, one of <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s original producers, has stated, it was difficult to find adults who could identify a preschooler's interest level. Fifteen writers a year worked on the show's scripts, but very few lasted longer than one season. <a href="/wiki/Norman_Stiles" title="Norman Stiles">Norman Stiles</a>, head writer in 1987, reported that most writers would "burn out" after writing about a dozen scripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hellman-52_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-hellman-52-12">[11]</a></sup> According to Gikow, <i>Sesame Street</i> went against the convention of hiring teachers to write for the show, as most educational television programs did at the time. Instead, Cooney and the producers felt that it would be easier to teach writers how to interpret curriculum than to teach educators how to write comedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-178_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-178-56">[54]</a></sup> As Stone stated, "Writing for children is not so easy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-178_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-178-56">[54]</a></sup> Long-time writer Tony Geiss agreed, stating in 2009, "It's not an easy show to write. You have to know the characters and the format and how to teach and be funny at the same time, which is a big, ambidextrous stunt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-174_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-174-57">[55]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG"><img alt="Facade of a large white building, the left having large pillars beneath a strip with dozens of windows and the right three stories of large windows." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2204" data-file-width="2588" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG/200px-Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG/300px-Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG/400px-Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaufman_Univ_Studio_LIC_jeh.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Kaufman_Astoria_Studios" title="Kaufman Astoria Studios">Kaufman Astoria Studios</a>, where <i>Sesame Street</i> is taped.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The show's research team developed an annotated document, or "Writer's Notebook", which served as a bridge between the show's curriculum goals and script development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-101_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-101-58">[56]</a></sup> The notebook was a compilation of programming ideas designed to teach specific curriculum points,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-82_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-82-59">[57]</a></sup> provided extended definitions of curriculum goals, and assisted the writers and producers in translating the goals into televised material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer-10_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-10-60">[58]</a></sup> Suggestions in the notebook were free of references to specific characters and contexts on the show so that they could be implemented as openly and flexibly as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer_.26_Fisch.2C_p._11_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer_.26_Fisch.2C_p._11-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The research team, in a series of meetings with the writers, also developed "a curriculum sheet" that described the show's goals and priorities for each season. After receiving the curriculum focus and goals for the season, the writers met to discuss ideas and story arcs for the characters, and an "assignment sheet" was created that suggested how much time was allotted for each goal and topic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-101_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-101-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser2-28_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser2-28-62">[60]</a></sup> When a script was completed, the show's research team analyzed it to ensure that the goals were met. Then each production department met to determine what each episode needed in terms of costumes, lights, and sets. The writers were present during the show's taping, which for the first twenty-four years of the show took place in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, and after 1992, at the <a href="/wiki/Kaufman_Astoria_Studios" title="Kaufman Astoria Studios">Kaufman Astoria Studios</a> in <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a> to make last-minute revisions when necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MURPHY2009_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-MURPHY2009-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apollo_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-apollo-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spinney_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-spinney-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[note 3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesame_Workshop_funding_sources" title="Sesame Workshop funding sources">Sesame Workshop funding sources</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sesame_Street" title="Music of Sesame Street">Music of Sesame Street</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_co-productions_of_Sesame_Street" title="International co-productions of Sesame Street">International co-productions of Sesame Street</a></div>
<p>Early in their history <i>Sesame Street</i> and the CTW began to look for alternative funding sources and turned to creating products and writing licensing agreements. They became, as Cooney put it, "a multiple-media institution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherow-197_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-cherow-197-67">[64]</a></sup> In 1970, the CTW created a "non-broadcast" division responsible for creating and publishing books and <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street_Magazine" title="Sesame Street Magazine">Sesame Street Magazine</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherow19798_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-cherow19798-68">[65]</a></sup> They decided that all materials their licensing program created would "underscore and amplify"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-195_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-195-69">[66]</a></sup> the show's curriculum. In 2004, over 68% of <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s revenue came from licenses and products such as toys and clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carvajal_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-carvajal-70">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> By 2008, the <i>Sesame Street</i> Muppets accounted for between $15 million and $17 million per year in licensing and merchandising fees, split between the Sesame Workshop and The Jim Henson Company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-5_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-5-72">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jim_Henson" title="Jim Henson">Jim Henson</a>, the creator of the Muppets, owned the <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademarks</a> to those characters, and was reluctant to market them at first. He agreed when the CTW promised that the profits from toys, books, <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street_video_games" title="Sesame Street video games">computer games</a>, and other products were to be used exclusively to fund the CTW and its outreach efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-203_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis-203-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-268_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-268-73">[70]</a></sup> Even though Cooney and the CTW had very little experience with marketing, they demanded complete control over all products and product decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-205_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-205-74">[71]</a></sup> Any product line associated with the show had to be educational and inexpensive, and could not be advertised during the show's airings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-204_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-204-75">[72]</a></sup> As Davis reported, "Cooney stressed restraint, prudence, and caution" in their marketing and licensing efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-204_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis-204-75">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Director Jon Stone, talking about the music of <i>Sesame Street</i>, said: "There was no other sound like it on television".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-220_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-220-76">[73]</a></sup> For the first time in children's television, the show's songs fulfilled a specific purpose and supported its curriculum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-227_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-227-77">[74]</a></sup> In order to attract the best composers and lyricists, the CTW allowed songwriters like <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s first musical director <a href="/wiki/Joe_Raposo" title="Joe Raposo">Joe Raposo</a> to retain the rights to the songs they wrote, which earned them lucrative profits and helped the show sustain public interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-256_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-256-78">[75]</a></sup> By 1991, <i>Sesame Street</i> and its songwriters had received eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammys" title="Grammys">Grammys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kohn_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-kohn-79">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Sesame Street</i> used animations and short films commissioned from outside studios,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-236_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-236-80">[77]</a></sup> interspersed throughout each episode, to help teach their viewers basic concepts like numbers and letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-89_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-89-81">[78]</a></sup> Jim Henson was one of the many producers to create short films for the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gikow.2C_p._246_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gikow.2C_p._246-82">[79]</a></sup> Shortly after <i>Sesame Street</i> debuted in the United States, the CTW was approached independently by producers from several countries to produce versions of the show at home. These versions came to be called "co-productions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cole-148_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-cole-148-83">[80]</a></sup> By 2001 there were over 120 million viewers of all international versions of <i>Sesame Street</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cole-147_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-cole-147-84">[81]</a></sup> and in 2006, there were twenty co-productions around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-world-85">[82]</a></sup> By the show's 40th anniversary in 2009, <i>Sesame Street</i> was broadcast in more than 140 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-263_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-263-86">[83]</a></sup> In 2005, Doreen Carvajal of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported that income from the co-productions and international licensing accounted for $96 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carvajal_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-carvajal-70">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cast.2C_crew_and_characters">Cast, crew and characters</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street_characters" title="Sesame Street characters">Sesame Street characters</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_guest_stars_on_Sesame_Street" title="List of guest stars on Sesame Street">List of guest stars on Sesame Street</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jim_Henson_(1989).jpg"><img alt="Jim Henson in 1989." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="487" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Jim_Henson_%281989%29.jpg/170px-Jim_Henson_%281989%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Jim_Henson_%281989%29.jpg/255px-Jim_Henson_%281989%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Jim_Henson_%281989%29.jpg/340px-Jim_Henson_%281989%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jim_Henson_(1989).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jim_Henson" title="Jim Henson">Jim Henson</a>, creator of <a href="/wiki/The_Muppets" title="The Muppets">the Muppets</a>, in 1989</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1735" data-file-width="1761" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg/220px-Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg/330px-Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg/440px-Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carroll_Spinney_and_Oscar_the_Grouch_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carroll_Spinney" title="Carroll Spinney">Carroll Spinney</a> and Oscar the Grouch</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after the CTW was created in 1968, Joan Ganz Cooney was named its first executive director. She was one of the first female executives in American television. Her appointment was called "one of the most important television developments of the decade".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis12829_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis12829-87">[84]</a></sup> She assembled a team of producers, all of whom had previously worked on <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_Kangaroo" title="Captain Kangaroo">Captain Kangaroo</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Jon_Stone" title="Jon Stone">Jon Stone</a> was responsible for writing, casting, and format; <a href="/wiki/Dave_Connell" title="Dave Connell">Dave Connell</a> took over animation; and Sam Gibbon served as the show's chief liaison between the production staff and the research team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-147_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-147-88">[85]</a></sup> Cameraman Frankie Biondo worked on <i>Sesame Street</i> from its first episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-15_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-15-89">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jim Henson and the Muppets' involvement in <i>Sesame Street</i> began when he and Cooney met at one of the curriculum planning seminars in Boston. Author Christopher Finch reported that Stone, who had worked with Henson previously, felt that if they could not bring him on board, they should "make do without puppets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finch.2C_p._53_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Finch.2C_p._53-5">[4]</a></sup> Henson was initially reluctant, but he agreed to join <i>Sesame Street</i> to meet his own social goals. He also agreed to waive his performance fee for full ownership of the <i>Sesame Street</i> Muppets and to split any revenue they generated with the CTW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-5_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis-5-72">[69]</a></sup> As Morrow stated, Henson's puppets were a crucial part of the show's popularity and it brought Henson national attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-93_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-93-90">[87]</a></sup> Davis reported that Henson was able to take "arcane academic goals" and translate them to "effective and pleasurable viewing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-163_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-163-91">[88]</a></sup> In early research, the Muppet segments of the show scored high, and more Muppets were added during the first few seasons. Morrow reported that the Muppets were effective teaching tools because children easily recognized them, they were stereotypical and predictable, and they appealed to adults and older siblings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-9495_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-9495-92">[89]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"<i>Sesame Street</i> is best known for the creative geniuses it attracted, people like Jim Henson and Joe Raposo and Frank Oz, who intuitively grasped what it takes to get through to children. They were television's answer to Beatrix Potter or L. Frank Baum or Dr. Seuss."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—Author <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Gladwell" title="Malcolm Gladwell">Malcolm Gladwell</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tipping_Point" title="The Tipping Point">The Tipping Point</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gladwell-99_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-gladwell-99-93">[90]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Although the producers decided against depending upon a single host for <i>Sesame Street</i>, instead casting a group of ethnically diverse actors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-99_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-99-94">[91]</a></sup> they realized that a children's television program needed to have, as Lesser put it, "a variety of distinctive and reliable personalities",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-125_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-125-95">[92]</a></sup> both human and Muppet. Jon Stone, whose goal was to cast white actors in the minority,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hellman-52_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-hellman-52-12">[11]</a></sup> was responsible for hiring the show's first <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_Sesame_Street_characters" title="List of human Sesame Street characters">cast</a>. He did not audition actors until Spring 1969, a few weeks before the five test shows were due to be filmed. Stone videotaped the auditions, and Ed Palmer took them out into the field to test children's reactions. The actors who received the "most enthusiastic thumbs up" were cast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borgenicht.2C_p._15_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borgenicht.2C_p._15-96">[93]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Loretta_Long" title="Loretta Long">Loretta Long</a> was chosen to play <a href="/wiki/The_Robinson_family_(Sesame_Street)" title="The Robinson family (Sesame Street)">Susan</a> when the children who saw her audition stood up and sang along with her rendition of "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_a_Little_Teapot" title="I'm a Little Teapot">I'm a Little Teapot</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borgenicht.2C_p._15_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borgenicht.2C_p._15-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-172_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-172-97">[94]</a></sup> As Stone said, casting was the only aspect of the show that was "just completely haphazard".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-195_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis-195-69">[66]</a></sup> Most of the cast and crew found jobs on <i>Sesame Street</i> through personal relationships with Stone and the other producers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-195_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-davis-195-69">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the CTW's research, children preferred watching and listening to other children more than to puppets and adults, so they included children in many scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-127_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-127-98">[95]</a></sup> Dave Connell insisted that no child actors be used,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-84_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-84-99">[96]</a></sup> so these children were nonprofessionals, unscripted, and spontaneous. Many of their reactions were unpredictable and difficult to control, but the adult <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_Sesame_Street_characters" title="List of human Sesame Street characters">cast</a> learned to handle the children's spontaneity flexibly, even when it resulted in departures from the planned script or lesson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-12728_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-12728-100">[97]</a></sup> CTW research also revealed that the children's hesitations and on-air mistakes served as models for viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-123_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-123-101">[98]</a></sup> According to Morrow, this resulted in the show having a "fresh quality", especially in its early years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-84_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-84-99">[96]</a></sup> Children were also used in the voice-over commentaries of most of the live-action films the CTW produced.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_Sesame_Street" title="Influence of Sesame Street">Influence of Sesame Street</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratings">Ratings</span></h3>
<p>When <i>Sesame Street</i> premiered in 1969, it aired on only 67.6% of American televisions, but it earned a 3.3 <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" title="Nielsen ratings">Nielsen</a> rating, which totaled 1.9 million households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seligsohn_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seligsohn-102">[99]</a></sup> By the show's tenth anniversary in 1979, nine million American children under the age of 6 were watching <i>Sesame Street</i> daily. According to a 1993 survey conducted by the U.S. Department of Education, out of the show's 6.6 million viewers, 2.4 million kindergartners regularly watched it. 77% of preschoolers watched it once a week, and 86% of kindergartners and first- and second-grade students had watched it once a week before starting school. The show reached most young children in almost all demographic groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zill-117_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-zill-117-103">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The show's ratings significantly decreased in the early 1990s, resulting from changes in children's viewing habits and in the television marketplace. The producers responded by making large-scale structural changes to the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weiss_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-weiss-104">[101]</a></sup> By 2006, <i>Sesame Street</i> had become "the most widely viewed children's television show in the world", with 20 international independent versions and broadcasts in over 120 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friedman_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-friedman-8">[7]</a></sup> A 1996 survey found that 95% of all American preschoolers had watched the show by the time they were three years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-growing_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-growing-105">[102]</a></sup> In 2008, it was estimated that 77 million Americans had watched the series as children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friedman_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-friedman-8">[7]</a></sup> By the show's 40th anniversary in 2009, it was ranked the fifteenth-most-popular children's show on television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-counts_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-counts-106">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street_research#Summative_research" title="Sesame Street research">Sesame Street research § Summative research</a></div>
<p>As of 2001, there were over 1,000 research studies regarding <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s efficacy, impact, and effect on American culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooney-xii_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-cooney-xii-53">[51]</a></sup> The CTW solicited the <a href="/wiki/Educational_Testing_Service" title="Educational Testing Service">Educational Testing Service</a> (ETS) to conduct summative research on the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mielke-85_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-mielke-85-107">[104]</a></sup> ETS's two "landmark"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mielke-88_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-mielke-88-108">[105]</a></sup> summative evaluations, conducted in 1970 and 1971, demonstrated that the show had a significant educational impact on its viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer-20_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-20-109">[106]</a></sup> These studies have been cited in other studies of the effects of television on young children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mielke-88_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-mielke-88-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[note 4]</a></sup> Additional studies conducted throughout <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s history demonstrated that the show continued to have a positive effect on its young viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[note 5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"<i>Sesame Street</i> [is] perhaps the most vigorously researched, vetted, and fretted-over program on the planet. It would take a fork-lift to now to haul away the load of scholarly paper devoted to the series..."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—Author Michael Davis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis-357_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-357-112">[107]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Lesser believed that <i>Sesame Street</i> research "may have conferred a new respectability upon the studies of the effects of visual media upon children".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-235_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-235-113">[108]</a></sup> He also believed that the show had the same effect on the prestige of producing shows for children in the television industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-235_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-235-113">[108]</a></sup> Historian Robert Morrow, in his book <i>Sesame Street and the Reform of Children's Television</i>, which chronicled the show's influence on children's television and on the television industry as a whole, reported that many critics of commercial television saw <i>Sesame Street</i> as a "straightforward illustration for reform".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-122_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-122-114">[109]</a></sup> Les Brown, a writer for <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i>, saw in <i>Sesame Street</i> "a hope for a more substantial future" for television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-122_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-122-114">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Morrow reported that the networks responded by creating more high-quality television programs, but that many critics saw them as "appeasement gestures".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-127_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-127-115">[110]</a></sup> According to Morrow, despite the CTW Model's effectiveness in creating a popular show, commercial television "made only a limited effort to emulate CTW's methods", and did not use a curriculum or evaluate what children learned from them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-130_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-130-116">[111]</a></sup> By the mid-1970s, commercial television abandoned their experiments with creating better children's programming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-132_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-132-117">[112]</a></sup> Other critics hoped that <i>Sesame Street</i>, with its depiction of a functioning, multicultural community, would nurture racial tolerance in its young viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-124_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-124-118">[113]</a></sup> It was not until the mid-1990s when a children's television educational program, <i><a href="/wiki/Blue%27s_Clues" title="Blue's Clues">Blue's Clues</a></i>, used the CTW's methods to create and modify their content. The creators of <i>Blue's Clues</i> were influenced by <i>Sesame Street</i>, but wanted to use research conducted in the 30 years since its debut. Angela Santomero, one of its producers, said, "We wanted to learn from <i>Sesame Street</i> and take it one step further".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gladwell-111_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-gladwell-111-119">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>As critic <a href="/wiki/Richard_Roeper" title="Richard Roeper">Richard Roeper</a> has stated, perhaps one of the strongest indicators of the influence of <i>Sesame Street</i> has been the enduring rumors and urban legends surrounding the show and its characters, especially those concerning <a href="/wiki/Bert_and_Ernie" title="Bert and Ernie">Bert and Ernie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roeper_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-roeper-120">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span></h3>
<p><i>Sesame Street</i> was praised from its debut in 1969. <i>Newsday</i> reported that several newspapers and magazines had written "glowing" reports about the CTW and Cooney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seligsohn_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seligsohn-102">[99]</a></sup> The press overwhelmingly praised the new show; several popular magazines and niche magazines lauded it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow119120_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow119120-121">[116]</a></sup> In 1970, <i>Sesame Street</i> won twenty awards, including a <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a>, three Emmys, an award from the <a href="/wiki/Public_Relations_Society_of_America" title="Public Relations Society of America">Public Relations Society of America</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Clio_Awards" title="Clio Awards">Clio</a>, and a Prix Jeunesse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-119_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-119-122">[117]</a></sup> By 1995, the show had won two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peabody_Awards" title="Peabody Awards">Peabody Awards</a> and four <a href="/wiki/Parents%27_Choice_Award" title="Parents' Choice Award">Parents' Choice Awards</a>. In addition, it was the subject of retrospectives at the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allshow_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-allshow-41">[40]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"<i>Sesame Street</i> is...with lapses, the most intelligent and important program in television. That is not anything much yet."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—<a href="/wiki/Renata_Adler" title="Renata Adler">Renata Adler</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, 1972<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser-165_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser-165-123">[118]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p><i>Sesame Street</i> was not without its detractors, however. In May 1970, a state commission in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Public_Broadcasting" title="Mississippi Public Broadcasting">Mississippi</a>, the state Henson was actually from, voted to ban <i>Sesame Street</i> because of its "highly integrated cast of children" which "the commission members felt ... Mississippi was not yet ready for".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missban_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-missban-124">[119]</a></sup> According to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children_and_Television" title="Children and Television">Children and Television</a></i>, Lesser's account of the development and early years of <i>Sesame Street</i>, there was little criticism of the show in the months following its premiere, but it increased at the end of its first season and beginning of the second season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lesser17475_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-lesser17475-125">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[note 6]</a></sup> Historian Robert W. Morrow speculated that much of the early criticism, which he called "surprisingly intense",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-3_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-3-127">[121]</a></sup> stemmed from cultural and historical reasons in regards to, as he put it, "the place of children in American society and the controversies about television's effects on them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-3_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-3-127">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Morrow, the "most important" studies finding negative effects of <i>Sesame Street</i> were conducted by educator Herbert A. Sprigle and psychologist Thomas D. Cook during its first two seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow14647_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow14647-128">[122]</a></sup> Social scientist and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_Start_Program" title="Head Start Program">Head Start Program</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Urie_Bronfenbrenner" title="Urie Bronfenbrenner">Urie Bronfenbrenner</a> criticized the show for being too wholesome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bigbadtv_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-bigbadtv-129">[123]</a></sup> Psychologist <a href="/wiki/Leon_Eisenberg" title="Leon Eisenberg">Leon Eisenberg</a> saw <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s urban setting as "superficial" and having little to do with the problems confronted by the inner-city child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrow.2C_p._98_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morrow.2C_p._98-130">[124]</a></sup> Head Start director <a href="/wiki/Edward_Zigler" title="Edward Zigler">Edward Zigler</a> was probably <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s most vocal critic in the show's early years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-147_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-147-131">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>In spite of their commitment to multiculturalism, the CTW experienced conflicts with the leadership of minority groups, especially Latino groups and feminists, who objected to <i>Sesame Street</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s depiction of Latinos and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow15758_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow15758-132">[126]</a></sup> The CTW took steps to address their objections. By 1971, the CTW hired Hispanic actors, production staff, and researchers, and by the mid-1970s, Morrow reported that "the show included Chicano and Puerto Rican cast members, films about Mexican holidays and foods, and cartoons that taught Spanish words".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-155_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-155-133">[127]</a></sup> As <i>The New York Times</i> has stated, creating strong female characters "that make kids laugh, but not...as female stereotypes" has been a challenge for the producers of <i>Sesame Street</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-142_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-142-134">[128]</a></sup> According to Morrow, change regarding how women and girls were depicted on <i>Sesame Street</i> occurred slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrow-156_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrow-156-135">[129]</a></sup> As more female Muppets performers like <a href="/wiki/Fran_Brill" title="Fran Brill">Fran Brill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pam_Arciero" title="Pam Arciero">Pam Arciero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carmen_Osbahr" title="Carmen Osbahr">Carmen Osbahr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephanie_D%27Abruzzo" title="Stephanie D'Abruzzo">Stephanie D'Abruzzo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Barnhart" title="Jennifer Barnhart">Jennifer Barnhart</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Carrara-Rudolph" title="Leslie Carrara-Rudolph">Leslie Carrara-Rudolph</a> were hired and trained, stronger female characters like <a href="/wiki/Rosita_(Sesame_Street)" title="Rosita (Sesame Street)">Rosita</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abby_Cadabby" title="Abby Cadabby">Abby Cadabby</a> were created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gikow-143_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-gikow-143-136">[130]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/220px-The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/330px-The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/440px-The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_crew_of_Sesame_Street_at_the_69th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sesame Workshop CEO <a href="/wiki/Gary_Knell" title="Gary Knell">Gary Knell</a>, Executive Vice-President Terry Fitzpatrick, and puppeteer <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Clash" title="Kevin Clash">Kevin Clash</a> (with <a href="/wiki/Elmo" title="Elmo">Elmo</a>) at the 69th Annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peabody_Awards" title="Peabody Awards">Peabody Awards</a>, in 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2002, <i>Sesame Street</i> was ranked No. 27 on <a href="/wiki/TV_Guide%27s_50_Greatest_TV_Shows_of_All_Time" title="TV Guide's 50 Greatest TV Shows of All Time">TV Guide's 50 Greatest TV Shows of All Time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tvguide_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-tvguide-137">[131]</a></sup> It also won another <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a> in 2009 for sesamestreet.org.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup> In 2013, TV Guide ranked the series No. 30 on its list of the 60 Best Series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup> As of 2016, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesame_Street_Emmy_awards_and_nominations" title="Sesame Street Emmy awards and nominations"><i>Sesame Street</i> has received 167 Emmy Awards</a>, more than any other television series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmys2016_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emmys2016-140">[134]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Season 44 (2013–2014) was the first time episodes were numbered in a seasonal order rather than the numerical and chronological fashion used since the show premiered. For example, episode 4401 means "the first episode of the 44th season", not "the 4401st episode" (it is in fact the 4328th episode).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Gikow, p. 155, for a visual representation of the CTW model.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most of the first season was filmed at a studio near <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a>, but a strike forced their move to <a href="/wiki/Teletape_Studios" title="Teletape Studios">Teletape Studios</a>. In the early days, the set was simple, consisting of four structures (Gikow, pp. 66–67). In 1982, <i>Sesame Street</i> began filming at Unitel Studios on 57th Street, but relocated to Kaufman Astoria Studios in 1993, when the producers decided they needed more space (Gikow, pp. 206–207).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Edward Palmer and his colleague Shalom M. Fisch, these studies were responsible for securing funding for the show over the next several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer-20_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-20-109">[106]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Gikow, pp. 284–285; <i>"G" Is for Growing: Thirty Years of Research on Children and Sesame Street</i>, pp. 147–230.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Lesser, pp. 175–201 for his response to the early critics of <i>Sesame Street.</i></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li>Gladwell, Malcolm (2000). <i>The Tipping Point: How Little Things Can Make a Big Difference</i>. New York: Little, Brown, and Company. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0316316962">ISBN 0-316-31696-2</a></li>
<li>Lesser, Gerald S. (1974). <i>Children and Television: Lessons From Sesame Street</i>. New York: Vintage Books. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0394714482">ISBN 0-394-71448-2</a></li>
<li>Morrow, Robert W. (2006). <i>Sesame Street and the Reform of Children's Television.</i> Baltimore, Maryland: Johns Hopkins University Press. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0801882303">ISBN 0-8018-8230-3</a></li>
<li>O'Dell, Cary (1997). <i>Women Pioneers in Television: Biographies of Fifteen Industry Leaders.</i> Jefferson, North Carolina: McFarland &amp; Company. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0786401672">ISBN 0-7864-0167-2</a>.</li>
</ul>
